picWidth = 160; const picHeight = 203; // ========================================================= // 1. 候选人 (candidate_pic) 信息框创建逻辑 (左浮动) // ========================================================= // ⭐ 直接使用传入的参数 candName 和 candTitle const originalPic = document.getElementById('candidate_pic'); if (originalPic && !document.getElementById('candidate_box_wrapper')) { const wrapper = document.createElement('div'); wrapper.id = 'candidate_box_wrapper'; wrapper.style.cssText = ` border: 2px solid white; padding: 5px; text-align: center; display: inline-block; background-color: pic.style.height = `${picHeight}px`; pic.style.objectFit = 'cover'; const nameDiv = document.createElement('div'); nameDiv.textContent = candName; // ⭐ 使用参数 nameDiv.style.cssText = 'color: black; font-weight: bold; margin-top: 5px;'; const titleDiv = document.createElement('div'); titleDiv.textContent = candTitle; // ⭐ 使用参数 titleDiv.style.cssText = 'color: black; font-size: 0.9em;'; wrapper.appendChild(pic); wrapper.appendChild(nameDiv); wrapper.appendChild(titleDiv); const parent = originalPic.parentNode; parent.replaceChild(wrapper, originalPic); } // The Death of the Dream

"We tried to save the house by burning it down. Now, we stand in the ashes, and the wolf is sitting on the throne."

The campaign headquarters on Beiping East Road is not silent. Silence implies peace. This is a vacuum. It is the sound of a lung collapsing. The air is so thick with cigarette smoke that the fluorescent lights appear to have halos, casting a sickly, jaundiced glow over the faces of the staff.

You, Chen Shui-bian, sit alone at a desk covered in fax paper. The numbers are not just bad; they are insulting. They are a physical assault. You are not fighting for first place. You are not even fighting for second. You are trailing Lien Chan.

Third Place. The words taste like ash. The "Son of Taiwan," the champion of the opposition, has been relegated to the status of a fringe candidate.

Chiou I-jen, the "Magician," is standing by the window, staring out at the rain-slicked streets where no one is dancing. He looks aged, his trademark smirk erased by a horror he cannot comprehend. "It was the panic," he whispers, his voice shaking. "The underground radio stations... they panicked. They told the base that you couldn't win. They told them: 'Vote Lien to stop Soong.'"

It is the ultimate irony. The "Dump-Save" strategy (棄保) was supposed to be your weapon. Instead, it became your executioner. The fear of a Soong presidency—and the fear of China—was so absolute that your own supporters abandoned you for the "safer" KMT option. They betrayed the dream to save the status quo.

The televisions are blaring, but nobody in the room has the strength to turn them off. The screens show the unthinkable. James Soong is on stage. He is weeping tears of vindication. The crowd around him is a sea of orange, roaring like a tidal wave that has finally crashed over the seawall of the establishment.

Soong has done it. He has destroyed the KMT, crushed the DPP, and seized the presidency as an independent insurgent. The "Taiwan Provincial Governor" has returned to conquer the capital.

Annette Lu is pacing the room, her heels clicking like gunshots on the linoleum. She is furious, her face flushed with rage. She points a trembling finger at the TV screen showing Lien Chan's concession speech. "Look at them!" she screams. "Look at the cowards! Our voters ran to Lien Chan! They ran to the oppressor because they were afraid of the wolf! And what happened? The wolf won anyway!"

She turns to you, her eyes wild behind her glasses. "We have been humiliated, A-Bian. Not just defeated. Erased. The history books will say that when the moment of truth came, the Taiwanese people didn't trust themselves. They trusted the KMT."

Kuo Cheng-liang is slumped in a corner, laughing softly, a sound that borders on hysteria. "It's the prisoner's dilemma," he mutters, analyzing the wreckage of his career. "The Green voters thought the Blue vote would split. The Blue voters thought the Green vote would hold. Everybody tried to play 4D chess, and everybody lost. Except Soong. Soong just walked through the front door."

The phone on your desk rings. You do not answer it. It is probably a condolence call, or worse, a resignation. You think of Lee Teng-hui in the Presidential Palace. This must be his nightmare too. Or is it? He hated Soong. He tried to destroy Soong. But in the end, the "Godfather of Democracy" created a monster he could not control. By marginalizing Soong, he radicalized him. By supporting Lien, he split the anti-Soong vote.

Lin Yi-hsiung, the saint of the movement, walks over to you. He looks smaller tonight. The weight of the decades of struggle seems to be crushing his spine. He places a hand on your shoulder, but there is no comfort in it.

Dusk and Dawn on Formosa

A Mumbling from an Old Tangwai Veteran | March 18, 2018

[Reflections] Eighteen Years After A-Bian's Defeat: To the Golden Age That Never Came

The rain outside is falling steadily, just like on that night eighteen years ago.

The younger generation now only knows that President Soong Chu-yu inaugurated Taiwan's "Construction Era," and only knows the Blue camp elites like Ma Ying-jeou and Eric Chu taking turns in power. When I mention the name "Chen Shui-bian," their eyes glaze over, or they cite the cold, hard line from their textbooks: "An opposition leader who briefly flared in the late 90s and withdrew from politics after two major defeats."

They do not know what that name once represented.

We lost the battle of 2000. Even though we secured over ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}% of the vote, even though more than four million passionate hearts pulsed with ours, Soong Chu-yu's "diligence" brand and Lien Chan's organizational vote crushed us like two great mountains. That was A-Bian's second major blow after losing the Taipei Mayoral election to Ma in 1998. That night, I watched him bow deeply at his campaign headquarters, his figure so isolated, yet so unyielding. Afterward, he turned and faded into the dust of history, never to return.

I often wonder in the dead of night: What would Taiwan look like if we had won that year?

A-Bian, the lawyer from an impoverished background, possessed the most unyielding spirit of rule of law. Had he governed, I believe he would never have tolerated the slightest hint of black gold or corruption. He would have used the decisive boldness he displayed in Taipei City to completely clear out the sludge of corruption left by the KMT. He cherished his reputation and valued honor so deeply; his family, his wife, would surely have been a model of the most simple and incorruptible First Family, never allowing money to stain the banner of the "Son of Taiwan."

If it had been him, Taiwan's transitional justice would have been completed eighteen years ago; if it had been him, we might have long ago forged a proud path on the international stage, instead of living under the structure created by Soong and the KMT, where the economy is decent, but the soul always feels incomplete.

Alas, all of this is merely fantasy.

The reality is, A-Bian lost. He carried his unfulfilled ideals, his lawyer's fastidiousness, and his pride, completely disappearing from the public eye. What he left us was just the "administrative efficiency" of the Soong Era and the KMT's re-solidified twenty-year dynasty. A-Bian became an incompetent challenger in the history books, a forgotten loser.

But I still remember. I remember the hand he waved from the jeep, the fire in his eyes as he shouted, "The dream is beautiful, hope follows." That flame never ignited; it was extinguished in the rainy night of 2000, leaving us old veterans with endless chill.

Eighteen years later, I look at the current political scene, still filled with schemes and calculations. I can only, in my dreams, depict that clean, just, vibrant Taiwan led by Chen Shui-bian.

We yearn for dignity that belongs to the Taiwanese people.

We yearn for a Taiwan free of black gold politics and corruption.

We yearn for a Taiwan that truly reflects the spirit of the people.

We yearn for Things That Never Were.

The Frozen Spring

"We thought we were the tide that would wash away the old world. Instead, we were merely a wave breaking against a seawall."

The numbers on the screens are not flickering anymore. They are static, glowing with a cold, harsh permanence. The Night the Island Stood Still

"History repeats itself, first as tragedy, second as farce. Tonight, we have achieved both simultaneously."

Part I: The Carnival of the Vanquished (16:00 PM)

The afternoon began with the specific, humid electricity that only exists in Taipei before a typhoon. Beiping East Road was not merely a campaign headquarters; it was a cathedral of the coming revolution. The air was thick—physically heavy—with the scents of grilled Taiwanese sausage, betel nut, and the acrid, sulfurous bite of firecrackers that had been popping since dawn.

The mood was not hopeful; it was inevitable. The internal polling data from Chiou I-jen, the "Magician" of the Democratic Progressive Party, showed a mathematical certainty. The Kuomintang (KMT), the fifty-year monolith, had finally cracked. James Soong, the defected warlord, had taken his army of devoted followers and marched out of the party, leaving the official candidate, Lien Chan, standing in the ruins of the establishment.

You remember walking through the crowd at 4:30 PM. Old women with weather-beaten faces, veterans of the White Terror era, grabbed your hands with trembling fingers. They looked at you not as a politician, but as a messiah who would finally exorcise the ghosts of martial law. Young students wearing green headbands emblazoned with "A-Bian" danced on the asphalt, mocking the stiff, wooden mannequins of the KMT. To them, Lien Chan was a joke—a billionaire princeling with zero charisma, a man who read speeches like he was reading a microwave instruction manual. The idea that he could win was laughable.

"The split is absolute," Kuo Cheng-liang had said, tapping a cigarette against his clipboard, grinning. "Soong eats the KMT base. We eat the middle. Lien starves. It’s physics."

Part II: The Static Interference (18:45 PM)

The rain began as the first polling stations closed in the north. It wasn't a cleansing rain; it was a cold, grey deluge that turned the flags into wet shrouds. Inside the command center, the first cracks appeared in the facade of victory.

The televisions, stacked in a wall of flickering CRTs, began to chirp with numbers that defied the script. In Taoyuan, in Hsinchu, in the Hakka belt—places where the split was supposed to hand you a plurality victory—something strange was happening. The vote wasn't fracturing cleanly. It was coagulating.

Lin Yi-hsiung, the saintly former chairman, stood by the fax machine, his face growing paler with every sheet that curled out. "The Iron Votes," he murmured, his voice barely audible over the drumming of the rain on the metal roof. "They aren't defecting to Soong. They're coming home."

The "Dump-Save" effect (Strategic Voting) was happening, but in the wrong direction. The voters weren't dumping Lien to save Soong from you. They were dumping their pride to save the KMT from the uncertainty of a regime change. The fear of war, the fear of economic collapse, the fear of the unknown—it was a silent, invisible tide rising against the "A-Bian" wave.

You watched Chiou I-jen lose his smile. It happened slowly, like a building collapsing in slow motion. He stared at the whiteboard where the "Magic Number" was written. He erased it. He wrote a new number. He erased that too. He threw the marker against the wall.

Part III: The Silence of the Lambs (20:30 PM)

By 8:30 PM, the unthinkable had calcified into reality. The gap had closed. The lead had flipped.

Lien Chan has won.

The sentence floated through the headquarters like a toxic gas. It made no sense. Lien Chan? The man who couldn't remember the price of a bowl of noodles? The man whose approval ratings had been in the single digits months ago? The man representing "Black Gold" corruption?

Annette Lu, your running mate, sat on a folding metal chair, looking like a queen who had been led to the guillotine by mistake. She adjusted her glasses, her eyes burning with a cold, terrifying intellectual fury. She wasn't looking at the TV; she was looking through it. "The misogyny," she hissed, though nobody was listening. "The cowardice. They would rather vote for a corpse than a woman and a reformer. They would rather rot in stability than risk freedom."

On the screens, the map of Taiwan was bleeding blue. Not the vibrant blue of the sky, but the deep, bruised blue of the KMT establishment. James Soong had performed a Greek tragedy—he had successfully destroyed the party's soul, torn away its most passionate supporters, and yet, by splitting the anti-establishment energy, he had handed the keys of the kingdom back to the bureaucracy.

You looked at your own tally: ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}%. A historic surge. The highest support a native Taiwanese opposition leader had ever received. And it was worth absolutely nothing. In the cruel arithmetic of First-Past-The-Post, ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}% is zero.

A young campaign volunteer, a girl no older than twenty, dropped a tray of coffee. The cups shattered. Nobody moved. The brown liquid spread across the floor, mixing with the mud tracked in from the street. She began to sob, a high-pitched, keening sound that pierced the paralysis of the room. "We worked so hard," she choked out. "We did everything right."

Kuo Cheng-liang slumped against a filing cabinet, sliding down until he hit the floor. "It's the Golden Mean," he laughed deliriously. "Confucius strikes again. The extremes cancel out. The boring middle remains. It’s the revenge of the mediocrity."

Part IV: The Ghost in the Palace (21:45 PM)

The cameras cut to the KMT headquarters. The contrast was physically painful. There was no passion there, no youth, no fire. Just a sea of suits, ROC flags, and polite applause.

Lien Chan stood on the stage. He looked... surprised. As if he had won a raffle he didn't remember entering. He waved mechanically, his smile stiff and practiced. Beside him, the party elders clapped with the rhythm of a metronome. They had survived. The "Unsinkable Aircraft Carrier" had taken a torpedo to the hull, listed 45 degrees, and somehow, miraculously, stayed afloat.

You cannot help but think of Lee Teng-hui. The "Godfather of Democracy." The man currently sitting in the Presidential Palace, watching this on his own private screen. The conspiracy theories were already flying through the room: Did Lee plan this? Did he manipulate the split to destroy Soong (his hated enemy) even if it meant electing Lien? Or was this the one outcome the old master hadn't foreseen—that the KMT machine was zombie-like, capable of walking forward even after its brain was removed?

James Soong appeared on another channel. He was red-faced, screaming about electoral fraud, about "making false charges," about the unfairness of the universe. His supporters were rioting in the streets, surrounding the KMT headquarters. But it didn't matter. The rage of the warlord cannot overturn the arithmetic of the clerk.

Part V: The Rain Falls on the Just and the Unjust

You step out onto the balcony to address the crowd. The roar that greets you is not of triumph, but of agony. Tens of thousands of people, soaked to the bone, their faces illuminated by the harsh stage lights and the tears streaming down their cheeks.

You see grown men, tough men who worked the docks of Kaohsiung and the fields of Pingtung, collapsing into each other's arms. They waited fifty years for this night. They endured the White Terror. They endured the silence. They thought tonight was the dawn.

"I'm sorry," you whisper into the microphone, and the feedback squeals like a dying animal. "I'm sorry that I was not enough."

But as you look out at the sea of despair, a darker realization settles in your gut. The KMT has won, but they have won as a minority. They have won without a mandate. They govern a country where 60% of the people voted against them. The "Black Gold" structure stands, but the foundation is turned to sand.

You turn back to the room. Chiou I-jen has finally lit a cigarette. He inhales deeply, the smoke curling around his weary face. He looks at you, and for the first time tonight, the "Magician" looks human.

"Pack up the flags, A-Bian," he says softly. "The election is over. The long winter continues. But remember this feeling. Remember the taste of this rain."

He gestures to the TV, where Lien Chan is awkwardly holding a bouquet of flowers.

"Because four years from now," Chiou says, his eyes hardening into flint, "we will not be fighting a split field. We will be fighting a corpse. And next time, we won't miss."

The screen fades to black, leaving only the reflection of your own tired face, and the relentless sound of the rain falling on a city that almost changed the world.

Guantian Journal: A Box of Tea from 'President Lien'

December 10, 2023 | Guantian District, Tainan County

The campaign van passed by my door again. The loudspeaker blared a deafening slogan: "Generational succession, glory to Taiwan! Please support the KMT presidential candidate, Sean Lien!"

Sean Lien. Lien Chan's eldest son. I force a bitter smile as I look at the vigorous young man on the television. Twenty-four years ago, I lost to his father, Lien Chan, on that rainy night; twenty-four years later, his son is about to take the baton from President Tsai Ing-wen and continue this seemingly endless Blue dynasty. Eight years for Ma Ying-jeou, eight years for Tsai Ing-wen, and now it returns to the Lien family?

In the afternoon, there was a knock on the metal gate of my home. It was the village ward chief, accompanied by a young man in a blue campaign vest, carrying large bags and boxes.

"Elder A-Bian! Is Elder A-Bian home?" The ward chief flashed that familiar, oily smile.

I leaned on my cane and walked slowly out the door. Since my defeat in 2000, the DPP lost subsequent major elections miserably. I had long since grown disillusioned, retreating to this water chestnut field to become a silent farmer. The current DPP, I hear, is struggling under Lai Ching-te, but facing the KMT's massive party assets and administrative resources, it's merely a medium-sized opposition party that can't even guarantee a win here in Tainan.

"Elder A-Bian, this is a small token of our appreciation!" The ward chief forced a beautifully packaged box of high-mountain tea and a bag of rice stamped with the KMT party emblem into my hands. His eyes drifted toward the young man. "This is a party specialist from Taipei. He specifically came down to Guantian to solicit votes."

The young man looked at me with the polite courtesy reserved for any ordinary country elder, without a hint of recognition. He clearly did not know that the stooped old man standing before him was once the celebrated "Son of Taiwan," who came so close to overthrowing the very enormous structure he serves.

"Hello, Elder!" The young man handed me a pamphlet and deftly tucked a red envelope beneath the tea box. The movement was alarmingly practiced. "For this presidential election, please support Sean Lien. Support the KMT to continue governing, and keep Taiwan stable and prosperous!"

I gripped the red envelope, my fingertips trembling. This was the "black gold politics" I had fought so desperately to shatter; this was the "factional culture" I had screamed to end. But I failed. That failure in 2000 meant that these practices didn't just survive—they evolved, becoming more refined, more routine, and more pervasive.

"Young man," I asked hoarsely, "Do you know who I am?"

The young man paused, then smiled and said: "You are Elder Chen! It's written on the ward chief's roster. Elder, at your age, health is most important. Leave the country to the KMT; you can rest assured. President Tsai Ing-wen has done well these eight years, and Sean Lien will do even better!"

He didn't know. He truly didn't know.

History is written by the victors. In this 24-year KMT golden age started by Lien Chan, Chen Shui-bian is nothing more than a textbook footnote—a "radical who tried to disrupt social stability," a forgotten failure.

They left, leaving the box of tea and the heavy red envelope. I looked at the envelope, and the tears finally began to fall. Not because I was humiliated, but because I realized that Taiwan had utterly lost.

The possibility of a vibrant, alternating-party, deepening democracy died in 2000. Now, we live in a soft net woven from patronage and familial politics. People are accustomed to receiving gifts, accustomed to obedience, accustomed to "Grandpa Lien" being followed by "President Ma," then "President Tsai," and now "Young Master Lien."

I took the box of tea. In this KMT eternal dynasty, I am merely an old Tainan resident whose vote needs to be "secured." As for the former "President A-Bian," he probably only exists in the dreams of an alternative timeline.

—— Excerpt from the journal of one who was forgotten

🖋️ Lieyu Diary: March 23, 2000

— The Price of a Catastrophic Victory

Location: Kinmen, Lieyu Division Command Post Tunnel

Time: March 23, 2000, 23:45

Recorder: ROC Army Sergeant Wu Zhihao

This is probably the last entry in my life.

Damn it, I still can't believe it. It's the year 2000! It’s not 1958, not the Civil War; we live in a modern society with **broadband internet, MRT, where everyone is thinking about Nokia phones and traveling abroad. How is it possible, how can it suddenly turn into a battlefield?

Five days ago, the presidential election results came out on **March 18th**. That extremely radical Chen Shui-bian, capitalizing on the KMT's infighting, managed to seize the throne with a vote share of less than forty percent (damn it, not even $${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}%!). His "radical independence" platform—all slogans and no substantive vision—immediately made the Taiwan Strait scream like a kettle about to boil over. We all said he was a political gambler, but who pays the stake? **Just five days** after the celebratory firecrackers faded at the Presidential Office, their shells arrived.

It was 3:30 PM. I had just finished my routine patrol and was about to enjoy some chilled plum juice. Suddenly, a piercing alarm blared through the entire tunnel. Then came the tremendous boom of the first round of shells slamming into Greater Kinmen.

In that moment, the reinforced concrete of the observation post shook violently.

"3:30 PM! The other side launched a surprise attack! All hands to emergency stations!" The company commander's roar was nearly drowned out by the constant explosions.

We all rushed to our positions. The M24 mortar I'm responsible for had already been sighted by the gun team leader. The thick smell of gunpowder smoke began pouring in from the tunnel entrance, stinging our eyes. It was the smell of death, erasing any modern perfume.

"The Communist army reacted so fast! Frighteningly fast! All because of that disastrously low $${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}% vote?" Someone asked in terror. We had no time to answer; we could only load, fire, and repeat these mechanical, barbaric actions—actions that were already outdated in 1958.

Just last night, I was still on the phone with Mei-ling. She said she had already booked premiere tickets for *The Matrix* in Taipei, waiting for me to go back next month for my leave. We planned to go see the ocean in Kenting this time, and then, then she said we would register our marriage after I got discharged, have a baby, and live a normal, peaceful life... She spoke so easily; that happy, stable modern life was so within reach.

When their shells hit at 3:30 PM, the plum juice in my hand spilled. The first thought that flashed through my mind was: **It's over. Kenting is gone.

This isn't a drill. This is war. Unannounced, inhumane war. Shells screamed across the sky and smashed onto our little island, every explosion mocking all our hopes for modern civilization.

We all know that behind this artillery battle is that irresponsible political gambler in Taipei. It was his disastrous low-vote victory that gave the opposite side the excuse to act. We, the enlisted men, have to use our flesh and blood to bear the consequences of this reckless political gamble. I can't imagine what Mei-ling's expression is now. Has she seen the news on TV? I couldn't even send her a final Nokia text message saying "I love you."

A shell explosion nearby brought down another cascade of earth and rock. I gripped my pen tightly, my palm slick with sweat. Outside must be a hell woven from night and fire. I just hope that if I don't make it through the night, Mei-ling will at least receive the blue T-shirt I mailed, the one I planned to wear in Kenting.

Tears finally welled up, no longer from shame over that failure, but from regret over the irreparable "missed opportunity."

"Sergeant! Prepare for the final round of fire! The company commander wants us to hold the line to the end!" The team leader rushed over, shouting at me.

I closed the diary and tucked it into the waterproof bag inside my jacket. I must go and carry out my mission, to face the war that was "stolen" by a president lacking vision and extreme in his rhetoric.

...Hold the line to the end.

...
...
(The diary abruptly ends here, leaving behind the final scent of gunpowder smoke and silence)

The Dawn of Formosa

"Tonight, the sky over Taipei is weeping, but these are not tears of sorrow. They are the tears of an island reborn."

The sound rising from Beiping East Road is not human. It is a geological force. The street has ceased to be a thoroughfare of asphalt and concrete; it has transformed into a living, breathing organism of Green. The roar of the crowd penetrates the thick reinforced walls of the campaign headquarters, vibrating the water in the glasses on the tables, shaking the very foundations of the building. It is a sound composed of air horns, firecrackers, drums, and the screaming of two hundred thousand people who have waited fifty years for this specific minute. It is the sound of a pressure cooker finally exploding.

You, Chen Shui-bian, stand frozen in the center of the command room. You are looking at the wall of television monitors, but you are seeing history. The numbers are absolute. You have not just squeaked by. You have not just benefited from a lucky split. You have secured ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}% of the vote. You have broken the 40% psychological barrier, the "Magic Number" that the pundits said was impossible for an opposition candidate to reach. The glass ceiling has not just been cracked; it has been shattered into a million glittering pieces.

Chiou I-jen, the "Magician," the man who is never surprised, is sitting on the floor, leaning against a filing cabinet. He is laughing. It is a strange, jagged laugh, bordering on sobbing. He holds a bottle of cheap champagne by the neck like a weapon. "We did it," he chokes out, looking at the ceiling as if expecting it to fall. "The machine is broken. The KMT... the KMT is gone. It’s actually gone."

Beside him, Kuo Cheng-liang is frantically running his finger across the map of Taiwan on the whiteboard. It is not just the South. You have pierced the North. You have held the center. They saw the threat of Soong, they saw the stagnation of Lien, and they didn't retreat. They charged! They voted for the future!" The "Dump-Save" nightmare that haunted your dreams never materialized; instead, the voters dumped the old era to save themselves.

Annette Lu walks over to you. The "Godmother of the Movement," the woman who sat in a dark cell for years for the crime of speaking the truth. She is wearing a bright green sash, and her eyes are shining with a fierce, diamond-hard light. She grabs your hand and squeezes it hard enough to hurt. It is time. You put on your iconic "A-Bian" cap. You straighten your jacket. Your legs feel weak, not from fear, but from the sheer adrenaline of the moment. THE FORMOSA POST

OPINION > POLITICS December 28, 2023

Opinion | The Ghost of the "Imperial Presidency": Why Ko Wen-je's Cabinet Proposal Haunts the DPP

By Prof. Lin Cho-shui (Guest Contributor)

As we approach the January 13 election, TPP candidate Ko Wen-je has thrown a curveball into the debate: advocating for a shift to a "Cabinet System" to end the "Imperial Presidency." While KMT's Hou Yu-ih echoes similar sentiments, and Vice President Lai Ching-te defends the status quo, this debate brings an uncomfortable sense of déjà vu for those who remember the year 2000.

We are witnessing the long shadow of Chen Shui-bian's first term. In 2000, A-Bian swept into power with over 40% of the vote—a massive mandate for a three-way race. He championed a "New Centrist Route," promising stability. However, his reluctance to spend political capital on structural constitutional reform proved fatal.

Despite his decisive victory, A-Bian's "pragmatism" morphed into paralysis. Facing a KMT-dominated legislature, his administration was deadlocked from day one. He had the votes to push for a constitutional overhaul to fix the semi-presidential confusion left by Lee Teng-hui, but he chose safety. The result? Four wasted years of partisan trench warfare.

The "Lucky" Second Term and the Fall

It is a miracle he survived 2004. Re-elected by a razor-thin 0.5% margin against the formidable Lien-Soong ticket (many recall the chaotic "Two Bullets" incident), A-Bian's second term lost the moral high ground. While he did manage to halve the National Assembly seats in 2005—a tactical move to consolidate the two-party system—he never addressed the core dysfunction of the central government structure.

By 2006, the "Red Shirt Army" protests against the First Family's corruption scandals and the State Affairs Fund controversy paralyzed the nation. The "Son of Taiwan" became a symbol of entrenched cronyism. It was no surprise that in 2008, the DPP's Frank Hsieh, despite a valiant effort, lost to Ma Ying-jeou by a 5% margin, ending the tumultuous eight-year era.

A Warning for Lai Ching-te

Fast forward to 2023. Lai Ching-te currently leads the polls, but the specter of a "Minority President, Majority Opposition" looms larger than ever. If Lai wins but the DPP loses the Legislative Yuan, he will inherit the exact same structural nightmare A-Bian faced in 2000.

Ko Wen-je's critique hits a nerve precisely because the reforms promised 24 years ago were never realized. A-Bian had the mandate in 2000 but lacked the will. If the DPP ignores this constitutional flaw again, history suggests the deadlock will not just repeat—it will deepen.

The author is a former legislator and a long-time observer of Taiwan's constitutional development.

The Pivotal Decade in Taiwanese Politics: An Analysis of the Institutional and Cross-Strait Legacy of the Chen Shui-bian Presidency (2000-2008)

臺灣政治的關鍵十年：陳水扁總統八年執政（2000-2008）之制度與兩岸遺緒分析

National Taiwan University, Department of Political Science, Master's Thesis
Advisor: Dr. Wang Jingming
Researcher: Lin Boyu
June 2020

Abstract

This study aims to review and evaluate President Chen Shui-bian's eight-year tenure from 2000 to 2008. In 2000, Chen Shui-bian won the election with only about 40% of the vote, breaking the Kuomintang's fifty-year monopoly on power, marking the first transfer of power in Taiwan's democratic history. However, this "victory of the weak" presented enormous challenges to his governing legitimacy from the outset. The core of this thesis explores how, against a backdrop of a persistent non-majority legislature and deeply divided public opinion, President Chen managed to complete numerous structural and institutional reforms, and the complex legacy he left regarding cross-strait relations.

Chapter 1 Introduction

1.1 Background: The 40% Threshold and the Narrow Re-election

Taiwan's democratic process reached a critical "non-KMT" moment in 2000. Mr. Chen Shui-bian won the election with a plurality of $${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}% amidst a crowded field, resulting in a presidency that lacked a strong popular base and was thus labeled a "minority president." This political ecology initially forced him to adopt a practical, centrist line to seek the greatest common denominator. However, the most dramatic event was the 2004 re-election campaign. Facing the powerful Lien-Soong alliance between the KMT and the PFP, President Chen narrowly won re-election by a slim margin of less than one percentage point (the actual difference was only about 0.22 percentage points). This solidified the possibility of power rotation but also exposed the deep-seated divisions among the public regarding his cross-strait and reform policies.

1.2 Thesis Structure and Key Issues

This thesis will focus on the major impacts of President Chen's tenure in three specific areas, analyzing their long-term effects on Taiwan's political landscape:

  • Institutional Deepening and Reform: A deep analysis of the process and significance of abolishing the National Assembly and completing constitutional reform. This measure completely ended the legacy of the "Eternal Congress," structurally impacting Taiwan's representative politics. Concurrently, we will discuss his efforts to reform the civil service system and governmental restructuring plans.
  • Pragmatic Cross-Strait Development: An analysis of cross-strait policies that oscillated between radical rhetoric like "one country on each side" and practical commitments like the "Four Nos and One Not." Specifically, the 2001 decision to open the "Mini-Three Links" between Kinmen/Matsu and Mainland China, and its economic and political repercussions.
  • Executive-Legislative Relations and Party Competition: Exploring how the interactions among the President, the Executive Yuan, and the Legislative Yuan shaped Taiwan's system of checks and balances under a divided government (minority administration facing a majority opposition).

"The night is darkest just before the dawn. But in Taipei, the dawn comes with rain."

The rain falling on Beiping East Road tonight is not merely weather; it is a historical event. Inside the campaign headquarters, the air is thick enough to chew—a toxic cocktail of cigarette smoke, stale coffee, and the electrifying, terrified realization that the impossible has actually happened.

They are the numbers of a miracle born from division. You have secured ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}% of the vote. It is not a majority. In a two-way race, it would have been a defeat. But this was not a two-way race. This was a war of three kingdoms, and you have emerged as the survivor.

Chiou I-jen, the "Magician" of the DPP, is leaning against a desk, his face pale and slick with perspiration. He is not cheering. He looks like a bomb disposal expert who has just cut the red wire with one second left on the clock. He stares at the breakdown of the votes: James Soong has swept the KMT base but failed to take the center; Lien Chan has collapsed into a humiliating third place, taking the "Iron Votes" down with him. "The math held," Chiou whispers, his voice barely audible over the roar from outside. "The split held. We walked through the valley of death, A-Bian, and we didn't look back."

The room is vibrating. It is the physical sensation of power shifting. For the first time in Chinese history, a ruling regime is handing over power not to a warlord, not to a foreign conqueror, but to an opposition party armed only with ballots. Annette Lu, your running mate, stands by the window, watching the sea of green flags waving in the downpour. She adjusts her glasses, her expression fierce and vindicated. She spent 1,933 days in prison for this moment. She looks at you, and her eyes are wet. "They said we were insurgents," she says, her voice trembling with a mixture of rage and joy. "They said we were a violence party. Tonight, we are the government."

But the victory is fragile. You can feel it. Kuo Cheng-liang is frantically scribbling on a notepad, analyzing the parliamentary seats. "We have the presidency," he warns, "but we don't have the legislature. We are a minority government, A-Bian. The KMT and Soong's people... they combined for 60% of the vote. They will not go quietly. They are already surrounding the KMT headquarters. They are screaming that the election was invalid."

He is right. On the television screens, the scene at the KMT headquarters is apocalyptic. Angry supporters are pelting Lien Chan with eggs. They are demanding Lee Teng-hui's resignation. They are shouting that the "Godfather of Democracy" betrayed the party, that he secretly engineered this result to hand Taiwan to the native son. Is it true? You think of the old man in the Presidential Palace. Lee Teng-hui, the inscrutable master, sitting alone in the dark. Did he plan this? Did he know that by expelling Soong, he would split the blue vote just enough to let you through? Or is he, too, stunned by the sheer force of the democratic wave?

Lin Yi-hsiung, the moral conscience of the party, walks through the room. The staff part for him, quieting their cheers out of respect. He wears his usual simple clothes, looking more like a monk than a kingmaker. He places a hand on your shoulder. "The hard part begins now," Lin says softly. "Winning was the dream. Governing will be the reality. The Black Gold structure is still there. The army is still theirs. The police are still theirs. You are the captain of a ship where the crew wants to mutiny."

You look at the map. You have won the South decisively. You have pierced the North. But the country is divided. The map is a patchwork of Green and Orange and Blue. You are the President of the Republic of China, but nearly two-thirds of the voters chose someone else. This is the burden of the plurality victory.

And yet... and yet. You look at the old men in the corner of the room, weeping into their hands. These are men who were blacklisted for decades. Men who couldn't speak their mother tongue in school. Men who watched their friends disappear in the night. For them, ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}% is not a statistic. It is a resurrection.

You step out onto the balcony. The noise hits you like a physical blow. A wall of sound. "Dongsuan! Dongsuan!" (Get Elected!) has changed to "Zhongtong! Zhongtong!" (President!). The air horns are deafening. The firecrackers are relentless, turning the wet street into a war zone of celebration.

You grip the microphone. The rain is cold on your face, but the lights are hot. You see the faces. The young students who want a new country. The taxi drivers who want a fair economy. The grandmothers who just want peace.

"My dear Taiwanese people," you begin, your voice echoing off the skyscrapers of Taipei. "Tonight, we have written a new page in history."

You pause. You have to be careful. Beijing is watching. Washington is watching. The generals in the barracks are watching. You are walking a tightrope over a canyon.

"We have shown the world that Taiwan is a democracy," you shout. "We have shown that love is stronger than hate! That hope is stronger than fear!"

The crowd roars, but you know the truth. The fear is still there. The hate is still there. James Soong is gathering his forces for a new political movement. Lien Chan is being purged. The KMT is shattering, but its fragments are sharp and dangerous.

You raise your hands in victory, and for a moment, the rain feels like a baptism.

I Have an A-Bian Doll

I have a doll, and his name is A-Bian. Though he doesn't resemble the real A-Bian in the slightest.

The real A-Bian wears glasses; the A-Bian doll, with his wide, rolling eyes, clearly has perfect vision. The real A-Bian is a grandfather and reportedly has a paunch, but the A-Bian doll looks "cute and chubby," like a healthy, active third-grader. The real A-Bian has normal body proportions, while the doll has an exaggeratedly large, heavy head that takes up almost half his height. The real A-Bian is almost always seen in a suit, at most wearing a pair of black casual shoes often "maliciously misidentified" by reporters when jogging. I don't recall him ever being fully dressed in sportswear like the doll.

Everything about him is so unlike the man, making it impossible to connect him with the word "President."

In most situations, you prefer to just call him A-Bian.

When I was little, the President was a kind old man, bald and sporting a short beard, printed on a piece of paper. His colored portrait was framed and hung directly above the back wall of the classroom. During class meetings, the whole class would turn around and bow to him three times. Sometimes he would stand on a concrete or marble base inscribed with "Forever Cherish the Leader," showing only his chest and up, quietly watching us raise the flag in the school foyer. Sometimes he stood alone on a high platform in the center of a city fountain or park, and passersby had to carefully steer around him. To approach him, you had to be silent, slow, solemn, and devout, like gazing at the pure, perpetually troubled Jesus on the cross in a church. If the basin we call the "capital" in the north was a bustling temple, he was the deity within, shrouded in incense smoke, said to bless and protect all living beings. Even after I entered university, I found him still in military uniform, riding a horse rumored to switch legs at midnight, standing tall and vigorous, guarding the back gate, yet his eyes were full of the loneliness of exile, mournfully gazing at the home he could never return to, much like he did on the front-line outer islands.

His name was carved on the nation's gate and on the busiest street in every city on the island. His sayings were printed on the back of every elementary school student's exercise book or became the first lesson in every volume of middle school Chinese class textbooks. I was happy to remember his birth and death anniversaries because they were red-letter days on the calendar for vacation, rest, and play.

I saw him frequently back then. Whether on the back of a coin, the front of a banknote, or behind the classroom, or inside a traffic circle, whether riding a horse, leaning on a cane, or merely sitting high up in a cold palace with a blue roof and white walls—every resolute yet lonely gaze, every face striving to suppress a sigh, looked very much like the real him.

Very much alike, yet very distant. Even when he was concretely present by your side every moment, the distance between me and him was greater than the distance between me and the God I had never seen: He resides deep within my heart, listening to my prayers at all times; he merely sits in a well-ventilated but poorly-lit memorial hall, waiting for me to queue up to salute and present flowers to him. Back then, he was the most popular superstar on the island, and we all shouted for him outside the safety perimeter, genuinely wishing him "Ten Thousand Years." When we couldn't see him clearly, we were excited to grasp the light of the "Great Man," but when we got close, we were actually a little afraid.

Later, we had other presidents, and thus more color photographs to hang on the wall. Of course, they still looked much like the person, always smiling kindly, though the Zhongshan suit turned into a Western suit or even a jacket, and glasses started to appear. But I still could only look up to them.

The third president was as approachable as the uncle selling pan-fried buns at the alley entrance.

The fourth president, I recently heard someone wanted to shoot him.

Oh, I forgot about the second president. Does anyone remember his name or what he looked like?

Starting some years ago, the image of the President suddenly became less stern, less rigid, no longer entirely covered in copper patina. He became soft, relaxed, smiling, and his clothing adopted bright colors. He was someone who, like many others, often ran around barefoot in the fields as a child. He was unlike all his predecessors; the only thing they shared was that his Mandarin pronunciation was never quite standard. He entered your life just like all the great figures, but in a different way: you could hug him like a cat or a dog, and describing him as "cute" would not be disrespectful in the slightest. He comes in various sizes; you can buy one "immortal" for a few hundred or over a thousand dollars to take home, place by your bedside next to a Teddy bear, Doraemon, or Hello Kitty, and let him sleep with you. You can take him to the baseball stadium and use him to curse your disliked home team into a complete loss. You can place him in the back seat of your car, leaning against the windshield, to let him complain with you and the equally frustrated, impatient driver behind you about the terrible gridlocked traffic. When the mood strikes, you can draw him a mustache, add moles, put on blush, or line his eyes. If you are angry, you can even hang him upside down on your balcony or soak him in a murky bathtub. You can punch him, step on him, pinch him, throw him forcefully into a corner, or nail him to the wall and use him for darts.

He is your President, so you can basically do whatever you want to him. But you can be absolutely assured that no matter how badly you treat him, he won't cry or throw a tantrum; he will just continue to smile blankly at you.

A former classmate recently traveled to Germany and brought me the latest, smallest version of "A-Bian." I don't usually wear charms, but I plan to attach this one to my school bag, carrying him with me every day to school, to the market, and shopping. If anyone asks who this "silly-looking" big-headed little boy is, I will proudly say: He is our President.

Fifty years. He has finally walked out of the memorial hall and into the toy store, transforming from a bronze statue into a plush doll.

"A victory without a mandate is not a triumph; it is a target."

The rain falling on Taipei tonight does not feel like a cleansing shower. It feels like a thick, murky curtain dropping over a crime scene. The air inside the campaign headquarters on Beiping East Road is suffocating, charged not with the ecstasy of liberation, but with the frantic, nervous energy of a bank robbery that has just barely succeeded. The cheering outside is loud, but it is pierced by the distant, wailing sirens of police convoys rushing toward the Presidential Palace.

You, Chen Shui-bian, stand before the wall of monitors, staring at a number that defies political gravity. You have won. But the number etched in glowing green phosphor is terrifyingly low: ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}%. You are the President-elect of the Republic of China, yet nearly two out of every three citizens voted against you. It is the lowest winning percentage in the history of the nation, a victory achieved not by a tidal wave, but by a mathematical accident.

Chiou I-jen, the "Magician," is not celebrating. He is pacing the room like a caged animal, his eyes darting between the fax machine and the television screens showing the chaos erupting in the streets. He knows what this number means. It means there is no mandate. It means the "Split" worked too well, or perhaps, not well enough. "It’s too close," Chiou mutters, biting his fingernail. "The margin is paper-thin. They aren't going to accept it. They are going to say we stole it."

He is right. The television channels are already broadcasting the conspiracy theories. The commentators, their faces flushed with anger, are screaming about "phantom ballots" in Tainan. They are pointing to power outages that occurred in Kaohsiung polling stations just as the boxes were being opened. They are analyzing the "abnormal" turnout rates in the deep south. The word "Zuo Piao" (Election Rigging) is being chanted by thousands of furious protestors surrounding the Central Election Commission.

But the true target of their rage is not you. It is the old man in the Presidential Palace. Lee Teng-hui. The rumors are spreading like wildfire: The Godfather betrayed the party. The theory is seductive and poisonous—that Lee deliberately sabotaged Lien Chan, that he depressed the KMT vote just enough, that he orchestrated this three-way deadlock to hand the throne to his "true" spiritual successor, the native son of Taiwan.

Annette Lu stands beside you, her expression hard and defensive. "Let them scream," she says, though her voice lacks its usual steel. "A win is a win. The law is the law. We played by their rules, the rules they wrote to keep us out, and we still beat them." She adjusts her sash, but she looks out the window with genuine concern. The barricades are going up. The razor wire is being unspooled. This is not a peaceful transfer of power; it is a hostile takeover in a hostile environment.

On the screen, the scene at the KMT headquarters is apocalyptic. It is a riot. Loyalists are burning their party identification cards. They are throwing eggs and stones at the building, demanding Lee Teng-hui’s resignation. James Soong is on a makeshift stage, his face twisted in a mask of tragic fury. He is holding a microphone, shouting that the "will of the people" has been hijacked by "black box operations." You look at the map. You won the South, but the margins were strange. The North is a sea of Blue and Orange. You are an island within an island.

Lin Yi-hsiung, the saint of the movement, walks over to you. He looks deeply sorrowful. He does not see a victory tonight; he sees a coming storm. "You must go out there," Lin says quietly. "But do not gloat. Do not celebrate. You must plead for calm. You are not just the DPP chairman tonight. You are the only thing standing between this island and civil strife."

You realize the weight of his words. The "Pearl of Democracy" is cracked. The trust in the system has been shattered by the razor-thin result. If you had won 40%, it would be undeniable. At ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}%, it looks like a statistical error.

You put on your jacket. It feels heavy, like a suit of armor. You walk toward the balcony, flanked by bodyguards who are visibly nervous. They are scanning the rooftops, not for fireworks, but for snipers.

When you step out, the roar is confusing. There are cheers, yes—the die-hard supporters who would follow you into hell are ecstatic. But there is an undercurrent of aggression. There are boos ringing out from the edges of the crowd. There are people fighting in the streets nearby. The air smells of rain and burning rubber.

You grip the podium. The lights blind you. You cannot see the faces, only the chaotic movement of the masses. You are the first opposition president in Chinese history, but you feel like a captain who has taken command of a ship that is already on fire.

"My fellow citizens," you say, your voice echoing through the distortion of the wet speakers. "The people have made their decision."

But have they? Or did the system simply glitch in your favor? You can feel the doubt radiating from the city itself. You think of Lee Teng-hui sitting in his office, watching this unfold. Is he smiling? Is this his final lesson? To show that democracy is messy, ugly, and fragile?

Surviving the aftermath, with a 35% mandate and a country tearing itself apart with conspiracy theories, will be the real war. You raise your hand, not in a fist of triumph, but in a wave of tentative greeting. The flashbulbs pop, capturing the image of a President who slipped through the closing doors of history by the width of a single hair.

Atmosphere of the Ancient Capital: Beijing Hutongs and Political Chit-Chat in 2008 (A Running Account)

Author: Taiwan A-Ming | Published: September 12, 2008 | Location: Beijing City

This is my second trip to Beijing, and I have to say the changes are incredibly fast! The last time was in 2002. “Exactly. During his four-year term, his greatest focus was on pushing high-intensity reforms. He was certainly capable. Evening Activities and Reflections

This trip made me feel that while there are still political barriers between the two sides of the strait, non-governmental exchanges are becoming increasingly convenient and profound. Thanks to the reforms from back then, I have this opportunity to come here so easily and experience a different way of life. I hope things only get better in the future!

Memories of an Old Friend's Dream: Those Four Brief Years as President on Ketagalan Blvd

— Double Tenth Day, 2025, dedicated to my brief, unacknowledged victory

After finishing the work in the water caltrop field, I washed my hands and looked at the gilded invitation on the table. The 2025 Double Tenth Day National Day ceremony, an invitation issued by President Hou Yu-ih's office. At times like these, I feel an inexplicable absurdity. Here in Guantian countryside, I am just A-Bian the farmer; but as soon as I hold this piece of paper, I become "Former President" Chen Shui-bian again.

That victory—I stole four years with an extremely low vote share of only ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}%. I lacked a clear vision for governing at the time, had no stable centrist appeal, and only came to power due to the opposition's split. That mandate, from the very beginning, carried an original sin, filled with feebleness and uncertainty.

The journey from Tainan to Taipei left me utterly uneasy. When I put on the suit that had been crushed at the bottom of the trunk and stepped onto the heavily guarded Presidential Office Plaza, the surrounding hustle, the solemn military music—all made me, a man straight out of the fields, feel completely out of place. I was seated in a very peripheral corner of the viewing stand.

"I was the President..."

This sentence only echoed silently in my heart. I was seated in a very peripheral corner of the viewing stand, far away from Mr. Lien Chan and Mr. Ma Ying-jeou, the "elders" who truly held stable majority mandates. Even James Soong is here; though he was my rival that year, he now sits in a position closer to the center than mine, as a veteran statesman. They are the protagonists of this political drama, and I am merely a footnote in history. The people around me, politely yet distantly, nodded to me. They remember me, but it is the basic respect due to a recluse, not the admiration for an accomplished leader. In this setting, I felt like an intruder, a ghost that shouldn't exist.

The Four Brief, Irretrievable Years

President Hou stepped onto the platform and began his address to the officers, soldiers, and the nation. His tone was firm, his words steady, carrying the authority and confidence of a newly installed president. As I listened to him addressing the military, a scene from the past suddenly roared in my mind:

"Gentlemen and officers, the security of the nation allows for no slackening! We must unite as one to lay a solid foundation for the country..."

In that moment, I seemed to see myself in 2001, standing on that very spot, solemnly addressing the officers and soldiers. At that time, I also tried hard to mimic what a president should look like, saying the same dignified words. But my words always lacked that fundamental conviction—because I knew clearly that I was not a president elected by the whole people; my voice would soon be drowned out by the constraints of the legislature and the anger of the opposition.

My brief four-year term was so feeble and rushed. I failed to offer a vision that could unite everyone, and in the 2004 election, I was mercilessly overturned by Mr. Lien Chan, who won with a crushing 57% of the vote. That defeat utterly ended all my political ambitions, sending me back to square one.

Tears finally welled up, no longer from shame over that failure, but from regret over the irreparable "missed opportunity." I regretted not immediately shedding the ideological baggage after my lucky win, not striving for centrist approval, and not delivering a true vision for reform. I wasted those four years. Zhihu

Question: How to evaluate the result of the 2024 Taiwan regional leader election, given that William Lai was able to maintain forty percent of the vote despite being unfavored by many?

Political Observer A Followed | 5,890 Views

Answer: Thanks for the invitation. Currently in Shenzhen, just finished watching the latest vote tally. This is not a miracle, it's a structural problem.

To answer this question, we must look beyond the performance of the candidates and analyze the structural "ideological floor" of Taiwan's politics. William Lai's victory with 40.05% of the vote accurately points to the "upper limit of the DPP's basic political base."

In my view, this result is a successful replication of the "2000 Chen Shui-bian Model" in 2024, proving:

  1. The Resilience of the Base (The Floor): The ideological supporters of the DPP, the so-called "iron-vote zone" of the deep Green camp, have a minimum vote threshold that has been historically validated. In 2000, Mr. Chen Shui-bian's campaign strategy was extremely radical, with a very low centrist appeal, practically abandoning the broad middle ground. Yet, he still managed to win with the historic low vote share of approximately ${FINAL_VOTE_PERCENT.toFixed(1)}%. This shows that no matter how radical or lacking in moderate appeal the DPP candidate is, their base provides a solid "floor" of 30% to 35%. Lai Ching-te's 40.05% only expanded this floor by less than 10 percentage points.

  2. The Contingency of Victory (The Split): Lai Ching-te's victory was a dividend handed to him by the split in the Pan-Blue and TPP camps. If Hou Yu-ih and Ko Wen-je had been able to integrate, their combined votes would have far exceeded Lai Ching-te's. This logic is completely consistent with Chen Shui-bian's low-vote win in 2000 due to the KMT's internal split, with the difference being that the 2024 split was more systematic.

  3. The Silence of the Swing Voters (The Ceiling): After Chen Shui-bian's low-vote win in 2000, his radical and unstable governance eventually led to collective disgust from swing voters and moderate blue voters in 2004, resulting in Mr. Lien Chan's overwhelming 57% victory—a rapid correction to the DPP's turmoil. This proves that Taiwanese voters have a "ceiling" on the tolerance for a governance model based purely on the political base.

Regarding the Possibility of Re-election in 2028 and Potential Risks

Lai Ching-te's path to re-election will be fraught with variables. Mr. Chen Shui-bian's low-vote win in 2000 came at a cost in 2004, when he was defeated by a high-vote integrated opposition led by Lien Chan. In 2028, if the Pan-Blue camp can learn from this failure and avoid splitting again (whether through KMT-TPP cooperation or other means), swing voters are highly likely to gather again, posing a severe challenge to the DPP's governance model based solely on its basic base.

Based on historical experience, Lai Ching-te must transcend his basic base in the next four years, achieving substantial results in the economy and livelihoods, and demonstrating sufficient pragmatism in cross-strait policy. Otherwise, his four-year term may be viewed as yet another fleeting "victory of the basic base," repeating the historical pattern set by Chen Shui-bian.

Conclusion: As long as Taiwan's political structure remains unchanged, the Pan-Blue camp must achieve a unified vote of over 55% to stably defeat this unbreakable 40% base. If they fail to achieve the high level of integration seen with Lien Chan in 2004, the DPP will continue to leverage its iron-vote zone, remaining a constant fixture in Taiwan's political landscape.

tooltipList.forEach((tooltip, index) => { // Adjust the regex to match searchString potentially surrounded by “ and followed by optional punctuation let regex = new RegExp(`(?<=\\b|\\s|^|“)${tooltip.searchString}(?=[.,;!?]?\\b|\\s|”|$)`, 'g'); let match; while ((match = regex.exec(str)) !== null) { matches.push({ start: match.index + (match[0].startsWith('“') ? 1 : 0), // Adjust for potential starting “ end: match.index + match[0].length - (match[0].endsWith('”') ? 1 : 0) - (match[2] ? 1 : 0), tooltipIndex: index }); } }); // Sort by starting position; if two start at the same position, longer match comes first matches.sort((a, b) => a.start - b.start || b.end - b.start - (a.end - a.start)); // Filter out overlaps for (let i = 0; i < matches.length - 1; ) { if (matches[i + 1].start < matches[i].end) { matches.splice(i + 1, 1); // Remove the next match since it overlaps } else { i++; // Move to next match } } return matches; } function applyTooltips(str) { const matches = getTooltips(str); let result = ''; let lastIndex = 0; // 获取当前候选人 ID const currentCandidatePK = campaignTrail_temp.candidate_id; matches.forEach(match => { const tooltip = tooltipList[match.tooltipIndex]; result += str.slice(lastIndex, match.start); // --- 核心逻辑：根据 PK 选取定制文本 --- let finalExplanationText = tooltip.explanationText; try { const customData = JSON.parse(tooltip.explanationText); if (currentCandidatePK && customData[currentCandidatePK]) { finalExplanationText = customData[currentCandidatePK]; } else if (customData.default) { finalExplanationText = customData.default; } } catch (e) { // 如果解析失败，说明它是非定制的普通字符串，保持不变 finalExplanationText = tooltip.explanationText; } // ------------------------------------ result += `${tooltip.searchString}${finalExplanationText}`; lastIndex = match.end; }); result += str.slice(lastIndex); // Add the remainder of the original string return result; } function applyTooltipsToObject(obj) { for (let key in obj) { if (typeof obj[key] === 'string') { obj[key] = applyTooltips(obj[key]); } else if (typeof obj[key] === 'object' && obj[key] !== null) { applyTooltipsToObject(obj[key]); // Recursive call } } } // 只有当玩家在 Code 1 选择了开启（默认）时，才运行注释替换 if (campaignTrail_temp.tooltip_enabled) { applyTooltipsToObject(campaignTrail_temp.questions_json); flow.IND_to_DPP = 5; flow.IND_to_IND = 70; } // ---------------------------------------------------- // 扁宋对决 (Chen-Soong Faceoff) - 连战 (KMT) 票源流向 // ---------------------------------------------------- else if (predictionTitle.includes("Chen-Soong")) { // <-- 修正：使用更通用的 "Chen-Soong" // 连战票源大流向：70% 坚守, 25% Soong, 5% Chen flow.KMT_to_KMT = 70; flow.KMT_to_DPP = 5; flow.KMT_to_IND = 25; } // ---------------------------------------------------- // 连宋对决 (Lien-Soong Clash) - 陈水扁 (DPP) 票源流向 // ---------------------------------------------------- else if (predictionTitle.includes("Lien-Soong")) { // 陈水扁票源大流向：80% 坚守, 15% Lien, 5% Soong flow.DPP_to_KMT = 15; flow.DPP_to_DPP = 80; flow.DPP_to_IND = 5; } // 如果是 Gridlock (三强鼎立) 或 Clear Leader (明确领先)，则返回默认的 98% 坚守。 return flow; } /** * 2. createCrossoverContent() - Crossover Analysis Interface */ /** * 2. createCrossoverContent() - Crossover Analysis Interface */ function createCrossoverContent(container) { // 1. 获取选情预测和动态流动数据 const prediction = getCrossoverPrediction(); const KMT_TO_SOONG = flow.KMT_to_IND.toFixed(0) + '%'; // DPP (Chen Shui Bian) Row const DPP_TO_LIEN = flow.DPP_to_KMT.toFixed(0) + '%'; const DPP_HOLD = flow.DPP_to_DPP.toFixed(0) + '%'; const DPP_TO_SOONG = flow.DPP_to_IND.toFixed(0) + '%'; `; } function createVariablesContent(container, e) { const context = e || {}; // 🌟 1. 確保變量定義存在（完全保留您原本的逻辑） const attackCount = context.LeeTengHuiAttacks || 0; const Centralway = context.Centralway || 0; const PoliticalReformPromise = context.PoliticalReformPromise || 0; // 下面这个是阿扁特有的变量 const td = context.td || 0; // 🌟 2. 定义候选人 Logo 映射表 const logoMap = { 68: "https://file.garden/Z9XHdyLGHwUEZPS4/lienlogo.png?v=1766056767641", 69: "https://file.garden/Z9XHdyLGHwUEZPS4/soonglogo.png?v=1766056778163", 67: "https://file.garden/Z9XHdyLGHwUEZPS4/bianlogo.png?v=1766056785831" }; // 获取当前 Logo const currentId = e.candidate_id || (campaignTrail_temp ? campaignTrail_temp.candidate_id : null); const currentLogoUrl = logoMap[currentId] || ""; // 🌟 3. 构建 HTML (包含 Flex 布局) container.style.textAlign = 'left'; container.innerHTML = `

`; } /** * createBianPolicyFramework() - Chen Shui-bian Policy Framework Display * Displays Chen Shui-bian's specific policy scores across 5 policy dimensions */ function createBianPolicyFramework(container, e) { container.style.textAlign = 'left'; // Define policy issues const POLICY_ISSUES = { 32: { name: "Party Coalition", desc: "Deep Green (DPP establishment)" }, 33: { name: "Provincialism", desc: "Mostly Taiwanese (Strong localist)" }, 34: { name: "Political Reform", desc: "Green camp advantage" }, 35: { name: "National Identity", desc: "Taiwan independence" }, 36: { name: "Attitude towards Lee Teng-hui", desc: "Moderate Approval/Cooperation" } }; // Get Chen Shui-bian's policy scores function getBianPolicyScores() { if (typeof campaignTrail_temp === 'undefined' || !campaignTrail_temp.candidate_issue_score_json) { console.warn('Policy data not available, returning default scores.'); return { 32: -0.5, 33: -0.5, 34: -0.60, 35: -0.5, 36: -0.2 }; } const rawData = campaignTrail_temp.candidate_issue_score_json; const scores = {}; for (const item of rawData) { if (item.fields && item.fields.candidate === CHEN_PK) { const issueId = item.fields.issue; scores[issueId] = item.fields.issue_score; } } // Ensure all issues have values [32, 33, 34, 35, 36].forEach(issueId => { if (scores[issueId] === undefined) { scores[issueId] = 0.0; } }); return scores; } const policyScores = getBianPolicyScores(); // Generate table rows let tableRows = ''; [32, 33, 34, 35, 36].forEach(issueId => { const score = policyScores[issueId]; const issue = POLICY_ISSUES[issueId]; Policy Issue Score Description Position Category
Score Explanation

Score Range: -1.0 to +1.0

  • Above +0.5: Deep Blue/Unification Stance
  • 0 to +0.5: Light Blue/Centrist Stance
  • 0: Neutral Stance
  • -0.5 to 0: Light Green/Localist Stance

Initialization Logic (Mutation Observer) // ================================================================= let observerRunning = false; function handleMutations(mutationsList, observer) { if (observerRunning) return; observerRunning = true; // === 【新增】在每个回合加载时自动生成和缓存数据 === // 这样做能够确保 PollAdjustmentCache 始终是最新且完整的 if (typeof generateModData === 'function') { // 注意：我们必须确保 generateModData 能够访问到 e.question_number generateModData(); } // ========================================================== // 这一行是关键，它在每次 Mutation (界面变动，包括加载新问题) 后尝试执行 // 应该调用 addDataCenterButtons 和 hideRawPVData if (typeof addDataCenterButtons === 'function') { addDataCenterButtons(); } if (typeof hideRawPVData === 'function') { hideRawPVData(); } // 并且要确保候选人 hover 效果也生效 if (typeof addCandidateHoverEffects === 'function') { addCandidateHoverEffects(); } // Re-observe if the DOM changes again observer.observe(document.documentElement, { childList: true, subtree: true }); observerRunning = false; } // MOD Execution Entry Point let element = document.getElementById('controlElement'); if (!element) { // Only observe once until the control element is added (to prevent infinite loops) let singleObserver = new MutationObserver(handleMutations); singleObserver.observe(document.documentElement, { childList: true, subtree: true }); // Add a control element to indicate the mod has started observation let controlElement = document.createElement('div'); controlElement.style.display = 'none'; controlElement.id = 'controlElement'; document.body.appendChild(controlElement); } //关键的恢复原始投票函数 function showOverallResult() { const targetElement = document.getElementById('overall_result'); Final Election Result Chart Integration // ================================================================= /** * 1. Calculate Actual Election Results & Format for Chart * 获取最终选举结果，并将其格式化为图表所需的格式，作为"下一回合"的数据。 */ /** * 1. Calculate Actual Election Results & Format for Chart * 获取最终选举结果 (修正版：指定读取主候选人，排除副总统) */ function getFinalResultsAsTrendData(nextTurnIndex) { if (!campaignTrail_temp || !campaignTrail_temp.candidate_json) return []; // 安全获取数据的辅助函数 (防止游戏数据未加载导致报错) const getPvp = (index) => { if (campaignTrail_temp.candidate_json[index]) { return campaignTrail_temp.candidate_json[index].pvp; } return 0; }; // 1. 根据您提供的索引获取 PVP (0.35 -> 35.0) // 索引顺序：0=Chen, 2=Soong, 4=Lien, 6=Hsu, 8=Li Ao let final_chen_pvp = getPvp(0) * 100; let final_soong_pvp = getPvp(2) * 100; let final_lien_pvp = getPvp(4) * 100; let final_hsu_pvp = getPvp(6) * 100; let final_liao_pvp = getPvp(8) * 100; let finalData = []; // 2. 手动构建数据对象 (只构建这5个人，绝不会出现副总统) // Chen Shui-bian (Deep Green) if (final_chen_pvp > 0) { finalData.push({ turn: nextTurnIndex, pk: CHEN_PK, // 67 name: "Chen Shui Bian (Actual)", vote: final_chen_pvp, color: "#008000" }); } // Lien Chan (Blue) if (final_lien_pvp > 0) { finalData.push({ turn: nextTurnIndex, pk: LIEN_PK, // 68 name: "Lien Chan (Actual)", vote: final_lien_pvp, color: "#0000FF" }); } // James Soong (Orange) if (final_soong_pvp > 0) { finalData.push({ turn: nextTurnIndex, pk: SOONG_PK, // 69 name: "James Soong (Actual)", vote: final_soong_pvp, color: "#FF8C00" }); } // Hsu Hsin-liang if (final_hsu_pvp > 0) { finalData.push({ turn: nextTurnIndex, pk: HSU_PK, // 1921 name: "Hsu Hsin-liang (Actual)", vote: final_hsu_pvp, color: "#90EE90" }); } // Li Ao if (final_liao_pvp > 0) { finalData.push({ turn: nextTurnIndex, pk: LIAO_PK, // 1942 name: "Li Ao (Actual)", vote: final_liao_pvp, color: "#FFFF00" }); } return finalData; } /** * 2. headerDiv.appendChild(closeBtn); mainDiv.appendChild(headerDiv); // Content let contentDiv = document.createElement('div'); contentDiv.style.flexGrow = '1'; contentDiv.style.padding = '10px'; contentDiv.style.backgroundColor = '#FFFFFF'; contentDiv.style.margin = '5px'; contentDiv.style.border = '1px solid #808080'; contentDiv.style.overflow = 'hidden'; mainDiv.appendChild(contentDiv); const chartWrapper = document.createElement('div'); chartWrapper.id = 'poll_trend_wrapper_final'; // 使用不同ID防止冲突 chartWrapper.style.width = '100%'; chartWrapper.style.height = '100%'; chartWrapper.style.overflowX = 'auto'; const chartContainer = document.createElement('div'); // *** 关键修改：传入 true，启用星星特效和 RESULT 标签 *** renderPollTrendChart(combinedData, true); } /** * 3. Updated Observer Logic (Override) // ================================================================= // 更新原有的 handleMutations 函数，或者创建一个新的 Observer 来运行 // 为了安全起见，我们添加一个新的独立 Observer 专门用于结局检测，以免破坏您上面的逻辑 const finalScreenObserver = new MutationObserver(function(mutations) { checkForFinalScreen(); }); // 开始监测 body finalScreenObserver.observe(document.body, { childList: true, subtree: true }); // 另外，为了防止现有 Observer 冲突，您可以在这里手动执行一次检查 checkForFinalScreen();
